Ruling on Stealing Registers and Ledgers
States ruling: no hadd except for account books

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for theft of registers and distinguish between content-focused and paper-focused items.

Core Idea
No hadd for stealing content; hadd for stealing paper when content is incidental.

This passage from al-Hidayah tackles a subtle distinction in theft rulings. The general rule is that stealing a register (daftar) does not incur the hadd penalty because the thief's real intent is the written content, which is not considered property (mal) under Hanafi law. However, account books form an exception: since the entry figures are not the target, the paper itself is the intended object, making it eligible for hadd if the nisab is met. Understanding this difference requires careful analysis of what constitutes 'property' in theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and General Ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on theft of registers (dafātir). The general ruling is 'no cutting'—no hadd penalty.
This is the default for all registers.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason is that the intended thing (maqṣūd) is the content (mā fīhā), and content is not property (mal) according to Hanafi fiqh.
Content includes writings, figures, drawings—anything that is not physically valuable.
3
Identify the Exception
The exception is account books (dafātir al-ḥisāb). For these, the content is not what the thief intends to take; rather, the paper itself is the target.
Account books are ledgers where the entries are not independently valuable to a thief.
4
Apply the Exception
Since the paper is the intended object, it qualifies as property. If the value of the paper alone reaches the nisab (10 dirhams or equivalent), the hadd applies.
Modern examples: blank checkbooks, official forms, archival paper—if their substance is valuable.
5
Generalize to Other Items
The principle extends: any item whose primary value lies in its content rather than its substance does not incur hadd. Conversely, if the substance is the intended object, hadd may apply.
Books of knowledge, personal diaries, and novels fall under the general rule; account books are a clear exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قَطعَ فِي الدَّفَاتِرِ كُلِهَا لِاَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ مَافِيْهَا وَذَالِكَ لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ إِلَّا فِى دَفَاتِرِ الْحِسَابِ لِأَنَّ مَافِيهَا لَا يُقْصَدُ بِالْاخْذِفَكَانَ الْمَقْصُودُ وَهُوَ الْكَوَاعِدُ
And there is no cutting in all registers because the intended thing is what is in them, and that is not property, except in account books—because what is in them is not intended by taking, so the intended thing is the papers themselves.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage addresses the theft of registers, ledgers, or notebooks. The general rule is that no hadd is imposed because the primary value is in the content (writing), which is not considered property (mal) for the purposes of theft. The exception is account books (dafātir al-ḥisāb): in such books, the content is not the intended object; rather, the paper itself is the target, and paper is valuable property. Therefore, if the value reaches the nisab, the thief's hand is cut. The commentary extends this to religious books, newspapers, novels, etc.—generally no hadd. However, official documents like property registrations or government secrets are considered property because the paper itself is valuable.

Key Terms
دَفْتَر (daftar): Register, ledger, notebook; a bound collection of pages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is there no hadd for stealing a book of religious sermons?
A) Because religious books are not property
B) Because the intended object is the content, which is not property
C) Because the thief had good intentions
D) Because the book is not kept in a hiraz

Key Takeaway
Hadd for theft depends on whether the stolen item's primary value is in its substance (paper) or its content (writing); content is not mal.

Ruling on Stealing Dogs and Cheetahs
States ruling: no hadd due to doubt in property status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of dogs and cheetahs and explain why the hadd is averted due to shubha.

Core Idea
Hadd is not applied for stealing dogs because doubt about their property status creates a shubha that averts the penalty.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hidayah that exempts thieves of dogs and cheetahs from the hadd punishment. The text provides two interconnected proofs: the existence of mubah (unowned) individuals of the same species, and the visible disagreement among scholars about whether dogs are considered property. Both create a doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd, following the well-established Hanafi principle that hudud are averted by uncertainties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being stolen? A dog or a cheetah. What is the ruling? No cutting (amputation) – i.e., no hadd punishment.
2
Examine the first proof: mubah origin
From the same species, there exist individuals that are originally mubah (not owned) and not desired. This creates uncertainty about whether any particular dog or cheetah is considered owned property (mal mutaqawwim).
3
Examine the second proof: scholarly disagreement
There is a clear disagreement among scholars about the property status (maliyya) of dogs. Some consider them property, others do not. This ikhtilaf itself becomes a source of shubha.
4
Combine proofs and conclude
Both proofs together – the existence of mubah individuals and the ikhtilaf – create a strong shubha. Since hudud are averted by any doubt (shubha), the hadd of theft is not applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَا فِي سَرْقَةَ كَلْبٍ وَلَا فَهْدِ لِاَنَّ مِنْ جِنْسِهِمَا يُوجَدُ مُبَاحُ الْأَصْلِ غَيْرَ مَرْغُوبٍ فِيْهِ وَلَانَّ الْاخْتِلَافَ بَيْنَ الْعُلَمَاءِ ظَاهِرٌ فِي مَالِيَةِ الْكَلْبِ فَاوْرَثَ شُبْهَةٌ
He said: And there is no cutting in the theft of a dog nor a cheetah, because from their species there exists that which is originally permissible (mubah) and not desired, and because the disagreement among scholars regarding the property status of a dog is apparent, thus it creates doubt.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that stealing dogs or cheetahs does not incur the hadd penalty. Two reasons are provided: (1) From the same species, there are animals that are mubah (not owned) and not desired, so there is doubt about their property status. (2) Scholars disagree about whether dogs are considered property (mal mutaqawwim) at all. Because of this ikhtilaf, a shubha (doubt) arises, and hadd is averted by doubts. The commentary mentions that some scholars compare dogs to pigs (non-property), while others consider them valuable. The existence of disagreement itself creates the shubha that prevents hadd.

Key Terms
مَالِيَّة (māliyya): Property status; being considered valuable property in law.
شُبْهَة (shubha): Doubt that prevents application of hudud penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd for theft not applied when someone steals a dog?
A) Because dogs are not valuable
B) Because there is a doubt about the property status of dogs
C) Because the owner does not guard the dog
D) Because the dog is not kept in a hiraz

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft is not applied when stealing dogs or cheetahs because uncertainty about their property status creates a doubt that averts the penalty.

Ruling on Stealing Musical Instruments
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the rationale for no hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi justifications for not applying the hadd penalty when musical instruments are stolen.

Core Idea
The hadd for theft is averted for musical instruments either because they lack property value or because the thief can claim intent to break them.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses the ruling on stealing musical instruments such as duff, drum, barbat, and flute. It reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute: the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that these instruments have no property value, so the nisab is not met. Abu Hanifa, however, considers them to have value but argues that the thief can claim he took them to break them, creating a shubha that averts the hadd. In either case, the outcome is the same: no hadd is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the instruments
The passage states that there is no cutting for duff, drum, barbat, and flute. Note these are instruments of lahwu.
2
Understand the two justifications
Read the reasoning: 'because according to them (Sahibayn) they have no value, and according to Abu Hanifa, the taker intends the breaking.' Recognize that the justifications differ.
3
Analyze the implications
For Sahibayn, the instruments are not considered mal mutaqawwim, so the value is zero. For Abu Hanifa, even if they have value, the possibility of a legitimate intent (breaking) creates doubt that suspends the hadd.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a thief claims he took a flute to break it, under Abu Hanifa's view, no hadd. Under Sahibayn, even without the claim, no hadd because no value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قَطعَ فِي دَفٍ وَلَا طَبْلٍ وَ لَا بِرَيْطٍ وَلَا مِزْمَارِلَانَّ عِنْدَهُمَا لَا قِيْمَةَ لَهَا وَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ اخِذُهَا يَتَأَوَّلُ الْكَسْرَ فِيهَا
And there is no cutting in a duff, nor a drum, nor a barbaṭ, nor a flute, because according to them (Sahibayn) they have no value, and according to Abu Hanifa, the taker intends the breaking of them.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage rules on theft of musical instruments (def, drum, barbat, flute). The ruling is that no hadd is imposed. Two distinct justifications are given, reflecting an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), these instruments have no value (la qima laha) because they are not considered property (mal) due to prohibition of their use; thus, even if taken, the value is nil, so nisab is not met. According to Abu Hanifa, these instruments do have value, but the thief can claim he took them with the intention of breaking them (ta'wil al-kasr), which creates a shubha. In both cases, hadd is averted. The commentary explains each perspective.

Key Terms
تَأَوَّلَ الْكَسْرَ (ta'awwala al-kasr): To claim the intention was to break the instrument, not to own it.
مال متقوّم (mal mutaqawwim): Property with legally recognized value; necessary for nisab in theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the accurate description of the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding theft of musical instruments?
A) Sahibayn say hadd is applied, Abu Hanifa says no hadd.
B) Sahibayn say no hadd because no value, Abu Hanifa says no hadd because of shubha.
C) Sahibayn say hadd is applied if the instrument is valuable, Abu Hanifa says no hadd.
D) All three imams agree that musical instruments have no value.

Key Takeaway
The hadd penalty for theft is not applied to musical instruments in the Hanafi school, due to either lack of property value (Sahibayn) or the shubha of breaking intent (Abu Hanifa).
